    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

        scope : 'name email',

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1649948179545395363"><strong><em>"Portrait in Sepia</em> is the best book Allende has published in the United States since her first novel of nearly two decades ago, <em>The House of the Spirits</em>.” <br/>—Jonathan Yardley, <em>Washington Post Book World</em> </strong><br/><br/><strong>“<em>Portrait in Sepia</em> tightens the weave of a multigenerational fantasy as complete and inspiring as the real world it parallels … Allende’s enchanting historical universe keeps</strong></span>
  <span id="freeText1649948179545395363" style="display:none"><strong><em>"Portrait in Sepia</em> is the best book Allende has published in the United States since her first novel of nearly two decades ago, <em>The House of the Spirits</em>.” <br />—Jonathan Yardley, <em>Washington Post Book World</em> </strong><br /><br /><strong>“<em>Portrait in Sepia</em> tightens the weave of a multigenerational fantasy as complete and inspiring as the real world it parallels … Allende’s enchanting historical universe keeps expanding and <em>Portrait in Sepia</em> is a new galactic jewel.” <br />—<em>Chicago Tribune</em></strong><br /><br /><strong>A sequel to <em>Daughter of Fortune, New York Times</em> bestselling author, Isabel Allende, continues her magic with this spellbinding family saga set against war and economic hardship.</strong><br /><br />Aurora del Valle suffers a brutal trauma that erases from her mind all recollection of the first five years of her life. Raised by her ambitious grandmother, the regal and commanding Paulina del Valle, she grows up in a privileged environment, free of the limitations that circumscribe the lives of women at that time, but tormented by horrible nightmares. When she is forced to recognize her betrayal at the hands of the man she loves, and to cope with the resulting solitude, she decides to explore the mystery of her past.<br /><br /><em>Portrait in Sepia</em> is an extraordinary achievement: richly detailed, epic in scope, intimate in its probing of human character, and thrilling in the way it illuminates the complexity of family ties.<br /><br /> </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=hispanic-culture">hispanic-culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=rereads">rereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=isabel-allende">isabel-allende</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594548354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11946032336266202196">Portrait in Sepia is Isabel Allende's middle book in a trilogy linking Daughter of Fortune to House of the Spirits. Sepia is the memoir of Aurora (Lai Ming) Del Valle, the granddaughter of both Paulina Del Valle and Eliza Sommers. The bulk of the story begins when Aurora is five years old, and Eliza Sommers, recently widowed, has decided to give Aurora to Paulina Del Valle to raise. Paulina ecstatic at finally having a girl to pamper agrees on the condition that Eliza no longer contacts the girl</span>
  <span id="freeText11946032336266202196" style="display:none">Portrait in Sepia is Isabel Allende's middle book in a trilogy linking Daughter of Fortune to House of the Spirits. Sepia is the memoir of Aurora (Lai Ming) Del Valle, the granddaughter of both Paulina Del Valle and Eliza Sommers. The bulk of the story begins when Aurora is five years old, and Eliza Sommers, recently widowed, has decided to give Aurora to Paulina Del Valle to raise. Paulina ecstatic at finally having a girl to pamper agrees on the condition that Eliza no longer contacts the girl. Within a year the Del Valles relocate from San Francisco to Santiago de Chile, creating a black hole of memories which Aurora carries for the rest of her life. <br /><br />The Chile that the Del Valles return to is much like the country we know in House of the Spirits- full of political upheaval and civil wars. Here we meet Severo and Nivea Del Valle, Paulina's favorite nephew and niece and the parents of Clara the clairvoyant. Severo and Nivea already have political ambitions here, and once Aurora is grown, Nivea becomes her closest confidante. I would have liked for Allende to provide a touch more backstory to Clara and her siblings so that the bridge to House of the Spirits would have been seamless. Yet, this is Aurora's story, so we do not meet Clara, although she is born late in this book. <br /><br />Paulina raises Aurora in a pampered, cultured life, allowing her to be tutored at home rather than school. At age 13 she becomes the photography apprentice to Don Juan Ribero, and Aurora quickly masters the trade. Because Paulina is two generations in the past, she feels photography will damage Aurora's marriage prospects so they embark on a two year voyage to Europe, both for Paulina's health and to find Aurora a suitable upper crust husband.<br /><br />While in England, the Del Valles encounter the charming Dr Ivan Radovic. Paulina is captivated by his modern medical ideas but does not view him as "husband material" for Aurora because he is not of the upper class. Meanwhile at a societal ball Paulina finds Diego Dominguez of an old Chilean land owning family. Although his family appears backward thinking to Aurora, her grandmother practically forces them into marriage, against the better thoughts of practically everyone else concerned for Aurora's well being. In anticipation of the marriage, Aurora's black hole dreams return. <br /><br />Part III focuses on Aurora's marriage to Diego Dominguez and briefly touches on Paulina setting up Severo and Nivea Del Valle in a vineyard enterprise before her death. Like in the other Allende books I've read there is a twist at the end that provides the reader with an aha moment. There is still some magical realism although not at the levels of Allende's other books, and it is still the sweeping epic I enjoy full of many beloved character; however, after Daughter of Fortune, this was a bit of a let down. The story serves as a bridge to link Eliza Sommers to Clara del Valle/ Trueba and the bridge was not seamless. Some of the writing felt forced because Clara’s story had already been written. Yet, less than stellar Allende is still among my favorite and I’m glad that I read this as it became more than a posterity read. Should I read the trilogy again, it will most definitely be in chronological order. <br /><br />4 stars </span>
  <a data-text-id="11946032336266202196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877036849" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17433643434130618511">Audiobook narrated by Blair Brown<br/><br/>This is the third book Allende has written about the Del Valle family. Though it was the last published, in chronological order it falls between <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16527.Daughter_of_Fortune' title='Daughter of Fortune by Isabel Allende' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daughter of Fortune</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9331.The_House_of_the_Spirits' title='The House of the Spirits by Isabel Allende' rel='nofollow noopener'>The House of the Spirits</a>, but can easily be read as a standalone novel. The sweeping scope of this book takes us from mid 19th-century San Francisco to early 20th century Chile, and is narrated by Aurora Del Valle, a fiercely independent woman who followed her own destiny regardless of convention.</span>
  <span id="freeText17433643434130618511" style="display:none">Audiobook narrated by Blair Brown<br /><br />This is the third book Allende has written about the Del Valle family. Though it was the last published, in chronological order it falls between <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16527.Daughter_of_Fortune" title="Daughter of Fortune by Isabel Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">Daughter of Fortune</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9331.The_House_of_the_Spirits" title="The House of the Spirits by Isabel Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Spirits</a>, but can easily be read as a standalone novel. The sweeping scope of this book takes us from mid 19th-century San Francisco to early 20th century Chile, and is narrated by Aurora Del Valle, a fiercely independent woman who followed her own destiny regardless of convention. <br /><br />I love Allende’s writing. There is a decidedly Latin beat to the flow of her sentences. And her descriptions are full of sensory highlights – the reader smells the sea air, feels the quality of fine fabrics, suffers in the tropical heat, hears the cacophony of a busy marketplace, and tastes the herbs and spices of Chinese or Chilean cuisine.<br /><br />The characters are larger than life and run the gamut from a wealthy Chilean grand dame to a Chinese herbalist to an English butler and a Serbian physician. I greatly appreciated the family tree printed in the text version, and wished that I also had a map handy to help with the geography.<br /><br />While she is known for magical realism, there is little of that in this book, aside from a reference to the ghost of Aurora’s grandfather being ever present. I think I need to go back to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9331.The_House_of_the_Spirits" title="The House of the Spirits by Isabel Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Spirits</a> and read it again….<br /><br />Blair Brown does a fine job performing the audio version. She has good pacing and enough skill as a voice artist to clearly distinguish the many characters. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17433643434130618511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_36880705" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36880705" />
    <a title="Huyen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/693262-huyen"><img alt="Huyen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239582969p2/693262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36880705?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Huyen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Huyen" href="/user/show/693262-huyen">Huyen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/693262-huyen?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36880705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18059145274293908504"><br/>“only passionate people make interesting characters. Nice people with common sense only make good former spouses”. <br/>Keeping that in mind, i usually have high expectations to see rebels, outlaws, eccentrics in Allende’s novels. Much to my disappointment, she fed me with a horde of former spouses. That story kicks off with a character that belongs to the type i hate the most: a teenage girl incredibly beautiful but also endowed with a less than peanut-sized brain who didn’t do anything but daydream</span>
  <span id="freeText18059145274293908504" style="display:none"><br />“only passionate people make interesting characters. Nice people with common sense only make good former spouses”. <br />Keeping that in mind, i usually have high expectations to see rebels, outlaws, eccentrics in Allende’s novels. Much to my disappointment, she fed me with a horde of former spouses. That story kicks off with a character that belongs to the type i hate the most: a teenage girl incredibly beautiful but also endowed with a less than peanut-sized brain who didn’t do anything but daydream of a prince and then blindly fell in love with an asshole. Thank goodness, she died after the first 100 pages. The middle was alright, when Chile went to war with Peru and Bolivia and women fought for universal suffrage. But then the ending was again, frustrating with a boring character that repeated exactly the same stupidity: getting married with a guy who symbolized the height of blandness. All the interesting ones drift into the murky background, I want to see more of Nivea the suffragette, Pidena the dissident, Lowell the boisterous actress, but they all disappear fairly quickly. And I have a major issue with Allende: she can never leave her female characters alone. Somehow, they’ll get married or fall in love in the end no matter how strange the relationship might appear. But why? Maybe, that wasn’t normal in Chilean society. But isn’t normality the last thing I want to see from Allende? I’m afraid for Allende, ordinariness is mediocrity. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18059145274293908504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2284634706" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2284634706" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2284634706?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780066211619" href="/book/show/500094.Portrait_in_Sepia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=south-america">south-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=family-dynamics">family-dynamics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=strong-women">strong-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2284634706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1642741697640480916">Historical fiction covering 1862 – 1910, set mostly in Chile, that carries on the family saga started in Allende’s outstanding <i>Daughter of Fortune.</i> Aurora del Valle tells her life story, starting with her birth in San Francisco to Chinese and Chilean parents. A traumatic incident has erased memories of her early years and has resulted in recurring nightmares. She grows up in the privileged world of her grandmother, which enables her to initially avoid the social restrictions placed on women and </span>
  <span id="freeText1642741697640480916" style="display:none">Historical fiction covering 1862 – 1910, set mostly in Chile, that carries on the family saga started in Allende’s outstanding <i>Daughter of Fortune.</i> Aurora del Valle tells her life story, starting with her birth in San Francisco to Chinese and Chilean parents. A traumatic incident has erased memories of her early years and has resulted in recurring nightmares. She grows up in the privileged world of her grandmother, which enables her to initially avoid the social restrictions placed on women and learn the art of photography. Her marriage leads to disillusionment and growth. Through photography and writing, Aurora seeks to discover meaning in her life. She notices that perspective changes over time and memories fade like old photos. While the primary focus is on Aurora, Allende has provided a patchwork quilt of segments focused on the lives of other family members, involving politics, love, honor, sex, feminism, patriarchy, social mores, and marital trials. Themes include memory, identity, and the search for truth. <br /><br />The strength of this novel lies in its characters. They are deep and well-articulated. Some are eccentric and outlandish. Others are reserved and honorable. Many of the women are strong, intelligent, and ahead of their time in terms of struggling against the prevailing views that women should be silent and subservient. This novel is also rich in Chilean history, portraying the War of the Pacific among Chile, Peru, and Bolivia and the brutally violent internal political struggles. <br /><br />Written last, this is the second of a three-book series between <i>Daughter of Fortune</i> and <i>House of Spirits.</i> The beginning of this book catches the reader up on the major events of the previous story, so it stands alone, but I recommend starting with <i>Daughter of Fortune </i> (link to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2193302915?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">My Review</a>). Recommended to readers that enjoy family sagas, stories filled with eccentric characters, or explorations of identity. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1642741697640480916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2596010182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2596010182" />
    <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads"><img alt="Abbie | ab_reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545340790p2/53055819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2596010182?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie | ab_reads" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads">Abbie | ab_reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007123018" href="/book/show/837673.Portrait_in_Sepia">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2596010182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16640168424649848067">3.5 stars<br/><br/>The sequel to Allende’s Daughter of Fortune is well worth your time picking up if you enjoyed the first one, but I did think Portrait in Sepia was a bit lacklustre in comparison to its predecessor. There wasn’t the same amount of passion and vibrancy leaping off the page, but as it’s Allende you can be sure that there is still SOME passion!<br/>.<br/>She is such a natural storyteller that I always can’t help but devour her books in a day or two. She weaves her stories so well that everything feel</span>
  <span id="freeText16640168424649848067" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />The sequel to Allende’s Daughter of Fortune is well worth your time picking up if you enjoyed the first one, but I did think Portrait in Sepia was a bit lacklustre in comparison to its predecessor. There wasn’t the same amount of passion and vibrancy leaping off the page, but as it’s Allende you can be sure that there is still SOME passion!<br />.<br />She is such a natural storyteller that I always can’t help but devour her books in a day or two. She weaves her stories so well that everything feels immediate and present, even when you’re in 19th century San Francisco during the gold rush, or Chile during the civil war. Her sense of time and place is unparalleled. Plus, this one is translated by my favourite so far of Allende’s translators, Margaret Sayers Peden! I cannot fault her work.<br />.<br />I always love it when authors reference their other works in books, and I positively fangirled when we got a glimpse of Clara del Valle, the famous Clara the Clairvoyant from House of the Spirits!! No one writes family dynasties like Allende! Paulina del Valle was probably my favourite character, the majestic and imposing matriarch of the family. And of course, reconnecting with characters from Daughter of Fortune was wonderful.<br />.<br />But ultimately I didn’t find it quite as compelling as book one, maybe I preferred the characters in that one to the main characters here. Towards the end it felt like it lost its way a bit, and tapered off with not quite as big a bang as I was expecting.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16640168424649848067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_244289279" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/244289279" />
    <a title="Lamski Kikita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1439240-lamski-kikita"><img alt="Lamski Kikita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321835207p2/1439240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/244289279?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lamski Kikita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lamski Kikita" href="/user/show/1439240-lamski-kikita">Lamski Kikita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer244289279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11156864147375855954">I am not sure how I can review this book as a piece independent from Daughter of Fortune. I have to admit though, that I enjoyed the aforementioned more than the book I am reviewing, though they are closely related.<br/><br/>I will start with what I didn't specifically enjoy. First, there are all the details of domestic life. I really do not need to know all that much about it, personally; I just don't feel like it is a subject that interests me to an extent that I wanna read paragraphs at a time about it</span>
  <span id="freeText11156864147375855954" style="display:none">I am not sure how I can review this book as a piece independent from Daughter of Fortune. I have to admit though, that I enjoyed the aforementioned more than the book I am reviewing, though they are closely related.<br /><br />I will start with what I didn't specifically enjoy. First, there are all the details of domestic life. I really do not need to know all that much about it, personally; I just don't feel like it is a subject that interests me to an extent that I wanna read paragraphs at a time about it. Second, why skimp on the juicy stuff? The narrator just mentions something that could be so great if elaboration was to follow, but alas, it is just a passing note and she goes back to failed love stories; why not elaborate on the coalmine workers and the massacre in the plaza that she witnessed?? Knowing that something like that could have been in the book makes me upset! Third, the love stories. Way too many desperate love stories and triangles that sometimes made me feel like I'm watching a telenovela. Fourth, the protagonist: although she had a horrible experience as a child, and lived to be a great photographer, I still was not very attracted to her character; she is not the usual strong female of Allende books, but was rather naive, too forgiving, and so easily manipulated. Her reactions to her marriage, her desperation to have a child, etc just made me not like her at all. She also always had a rather bigoted tone, which bothered me a whole lot. But i guess maybe this is Allende's way of surprising us; you won't always get a different version of the same character, sometimes the people in my stories can be silly, even the women?<br /><br />Ok, so that aside, I can now express how happy I was to learn more of Tao Chi'en; he had left me with a sour memory after he fell in love with a woman because her feet were tiny in Daughter of Fortune, but he more than redeemed himself and is now one of my favorite fiction heroes. I was also enchanted by the bits of history that Allende always enriches her stories with, and this was was especially interesting because it was not only about Chile, but also about 19th century (and early 20th century) America; the great melting pot of cultures that was filled to the brim with prejudice, racism, and so much hate. I also enjoyed her paternal grandmother's enterprising character, who represents the strong matriarch in this book, much to my relief. I absolutely loved the character of the protagonist's teacher, who was definitely my favorite in this book; her strength, her daring, her political stance, and her zeal. She is the perfect Allende female character which, sadly, had a very small role in the novel. And of course, i can't fail to mention the usual beauty of words and brightness of descriptions that Allende never seizes to please the reader with. I am always transported to whichever place she describes: China town, Valparaiso port, the Del Valle castle, or just a barn. Oh, how can I forget that there is a clairvoyant girl with green hair in this book too?? <br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed this book. And I guess after rambling about what i didn't like about it I realize that there is a point behind this novel; it is a story of someone's life, not a story of a nation or a generation, and sometimes a novel is only about the protagonist, whether I fall in love with her character or not. Nevertheless, I can't wait to read something of Allende's that is more powerful.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11156864147375855954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I have actually upped the rating from 3 to 4 stars. This is because the first time I read it I did not realize it was part of a trilogy. I'd already read House of the Spirits decades before and Daughter of Fortune maybe 5 years before.   <span id="freeText14348522877655444520" style="display:none">During the Covid-19 pandemic my local library has closed. As I result I have been rereading books I already own and Portrait in Sepia is one of them. I have actually upped the rating from 3 to 4 stars. This is because the first time I read it I did not realize it was part of a trilogy. I'd already read House of the Spirits decades before and Daughter of Fortune maybe 5 years before. At the time I did not make the connection and the story alone felt incomplete.<br /> House of the Spirits is definitely the best of the trilogy, ( in fact I always recommend it for people just starting Allende) and it can absolutely be read as standalone, however Daughter of Fortune and Portrait in Sepia should be read together to be greater appreciated. They are not quite as powerfully moving as House of the Spirits, but still highly enjoyable. The cover description says all you need to know. Set in the same universe, Allende's newest Book The Long Petal in the Sea is also fantastic!</span>

    <a title="Stefanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1255266-stefanie"><img alt="Stefanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294757948p2/1255266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefanie" href="/user/show/1255266-stefanie">Stefanie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343334126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4370427797521159463">I read Daughter of Fortune at some point last year and found it very enjoyable. This book is sort of the sequel to it, although it really follows a different generation of the same family. We do get to wrap up the "ever after" story from the previous book, though it's not until the very end of this one. I did not find this book to be as entertaining as its predecessor. I can't pinpoint what exactly I didn't like about it, except that it felt sort of rushed, like Allende was trying to get too man</span>
  <span id="freeText4370427797521159463" style="display:none">I read Daughter of Fortune at some point last year and found it very enjoyable. This book is sort of the sequel to it, although it really follows a different generation of the same family. We do get to wrap up the "ever after" story from the previous book, though it's not until the very end of this one. I did not find this book to be as entertaining as its predecessor. I can't pinpoint what exactly I didn't like about it, except that it felt sort of rushed, like Allende was trying to get too many (tragic) stories into one book. I like her general themes- memory, women's rights, what defines true happiness- and her writing is good, though this translation I read suffered a few grammatical problems that bugged me. Commas! Commas! Also, the epilogue ties the title in directly with the rest of the book, and that kind of killed it for me. It's like when a television show has to use the episode's title in a scene and they totally botch it. That is exactly how this felt.<br /><br />I wouldn't necessarily advise anyone against this book, but it isn't as good as Daughter of Fortune and that book didn't really need a sequel anyway. Maybe one day I'll get motivated and read something of Allende's in Spanish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4370427797521159463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5229326-emma-deplores-goodreads-censorship?shelf=family-saga">family-saga</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159327590" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16918987390573946256">I love Isabel Allende's writing: I read Daughter of Fortune and House of the Spirits, and found this one good but not quite up to the quality of the other two; lacking a distinct plot of its own, it mainly serves to tie the other two (standalone) books together. Still, it was an enjoyable book: Allende's character development and writing style are excellent as always, and I'm in the camp that loves to use fiction as a way to learn about the history of other countries, which this one certainly pr</span>
  <span id="freeText16918987390573946256" style="display:none">I love Isabel Allende's writing: I read Daughter of Fortune and House of the Spirits, and found this one good but not quite up to the quality of the other two; lacking a distinct plot of its own, it mainly serves to tie the other two (standalone) books together. Still, it was an enjoyable book: Allende's character development and writing style are excellent as always, and I'm in the camp that loves to use fiction as a way to learn about the history of other countries, which this one certainly provides. <br /><br />Still, the structure was somewhat odd. I would describe it as three novellas tied together to make a novel: <br /><br />1. The continuation of the story of Eliza and Tao Chi'en (and company) from Daughter of Fortune. On the positive side, we finally get that love scene that went missing from the previous book; on the other hand, so many beloved characters come to tragic ends that I'm not sure bringing them back was a good thing. <br /><br />2. The backstory on Severo and Nivea (and company) from House of the Spirits. This was my favorite part of the book: I liked them in House of the Spirits and like them even more now, and as it turns out, there was a lot we didn't know! This is also the plotline where we get to read about 19th century Chilean wars, politics and such. <br /><br />3. The story of Aurora, the narrator, who does not appear in either of the other books. While not as well-developed as many of the other characters, she's the glue that holds the rest of it together. Her life is interesting enough, but the truth of the Big Terrible Secret from her childhood is obvious from quite early on (perhaps intentionally so), and she lacks the strength and character of the heroines in Daughter of Fortune and House of the Spirits. I didn't think that setting her up as the narrator worked very well; she winds up describing in detail conversations that took place before she was born, other people's sex lives, etc., and generally stretching credibility. <br /><br />Others have mentioned Paulina del Valle as the main character, and I think there's some truth to that (although I see her in more of a supporting role): she raises Aurora and is around for most of the book, and certainly has the stronger personality of the two. She's one of those trademark Allende characters who make fascinating character studies, but are hard to like. <br /><br />I would recommend this book, but primarily to those who have read some of Allende's other work and liked it; if not, I'd recommend starting with House of the Spirits, which is her masterpiece. Come back to this one once you've read that and Daughter of Fortune and are looking for the rest of the story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16918987390573946256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9734474356361867230" style="display:none">Read this book because I will be traveling to Chilé in April and am attempting to educate myself. Am somewhat familiar with Allende's writing and am looking forward to seeing her when she comes to Buffalo in April....but this book...: skip it.<br />It just doesn't work. I can't even accuse the author of trying too hard. I don't think she had her heart in it. The characterization was inconsistent and I found this very confusing. She would flesh someone out in a particular manner (colorful and bold)and then go on to say how shy and wall-flowerish he or she was. I would go back and re-read, thinking perhaps I had confused the characters, but no,they were described with very different strokes.<br />At the onset of the book, the narrator of the story alludes to much more than the story gives.<br />What a tease.</span>

<div id="review_2779283174" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Ann-Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58567012-ann-marie"><img alt="Ann-Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594830862p2/58567012.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2779283174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2957145243226057677">I bought this book years ago to give to my mother for Christmas. Somehow it got shoved into a cupboard, life happened and Abi got something else that year. I kept the book, unread intending to give it to her another time. More life happened.<br/> I decided to just read the darn thing, and pass it along to her as a good, already read book.<br/><br/>I always approach Isabel Allende hesitantly. I don't know why. I am as reluctant to begin reading her books as I am to give them away.<br/><br/>Perhaps it is the three dimensi</span>
  <span id="freeText2957145243226057677" style="display:none">I bought this book years ago to give to my mother for Christmas. Somehow it got shoved into a cupboard, life happened and Abi got something else that year. I kept the book, unread intending to give it to her another time. More life happened.<br /> I decided to just read the darn thing, and pass it along to her as a good, already read book.<br /><br />I always approach Isabel Allende hesitantly. I don't know why. I am as reluctant to begin reading her books as I am to give them away.<br /><br />Perhaps it is the three dimensional realism of her characters. They are so alive it hurts me in my soul. I want them to pour out of the book and tell me the story of their lives themselves.<br /> Allende does not waste people, either. A character just passing through in one of the first chapters can return toward the end and affect the lives of many. <br /> <br />I find myself swaying to the rhythm of her prose, as if I were in a little boat on a river. Boy, that is trite, but I actually caught myself reading and rocking during parts of the book describing Chile.<br /><br /> Speaking of Chile, Allende's love for her country shines through in "Portrait In Sepia."<br /> <br /> This is just a beautiful, sensual, exotic story of love and courage.<br /><br /> I say nothing of the plot, as usual. You can get that on amazon.com. I just tell you why this is a must read book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2957145243226057677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText13937091923761095134" style="display:none">Really happy I read this, it's my first Isabel Allende and it was so much better than I imagined. <div id="review_146541793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146541793" />
    <a title="Faith Justice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4243191-faith-justice"><img alt="Faith Justice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509839p2/4243191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146541793?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faith Justice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faith Justice" href="/user/show/4243191-faith-justice">Faith Justice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780066211619" href="/book/show/500094.Portrait_in_Sepia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243191-faith-justice?shelf=given-away">given-away</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146541793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8885127874754707145">This is a sequel to Daughter of Fortune, a bridge to The House of the Spirits and not quite as good as either. Portrait in Sepia is written as the memoir of Aurora del Valle, granddaughter of the intrepid Eliza Sommers and formidable Paulina del Valle from Daughter of Fortune. Isabel Allende uses her usual style of intense character portraits, lyrical descriptions and background history of Chile to tell us what happened to all our favorite characters from that book; but her heroine is a lack lus</span>
  <span id="freeText8885127874754707145" style="display:none">This is a sequel to Daughter of Fortune, a bridge to The House of the Spirits and not quite as good as either. Portrait in Sepia is written as the memoir of Aurora del Valle, granddaughter of the intrepid Eliza Sommers and formidable Paulina del Valle from Daughter of Fortune. Isabel Allende uses her usual style of intense character portraits, lyrical descriptions and background history of Chile to tell us what happened to all our favorite characters from that book; but her heroine is a lack luster vehicle. To me, Aurora came off as a "poor little rich girl." Sure, she was orphaned, but raised by people who loved and cherished her. She watched a horrible tragedy as a child that gave her nightmares, but lived a life of luxury and intellectual indulgence. Even in an unhappy marriage, she found refuge in sympathetic and loving in-laws who did nothing to curb her eccentric behavior or limit her in any way. Aurora takes up a modern hobby (later profession) in photography, rides in men's clothing, and has an affair--all with no, or even threatened, consequences. She faced little in the way of personal obstacles, which is the essence of conflict in a character-driven story.<br /><br />I'm not denigrating the psychological effects of witnessing a personal tragedy, or saying rich people can't be unhappy; just that it didn't make for compelling reading in this particular book, especially against the background of poverty and war that Allende provides. The most interesting parts, for me, were catching up with the characters from Daughter of Fortune and seeing the seeds planted for The House of the Spirits(both of which I very much enjoyed). There are several more engaging characters than Aurora, which make the book worth reading; including Severo and Nivea del Valle, the parents of the main characters in The House of the Spirits. The Chilean history is also interesting and well integrated. I would recommend Portrait in Sepia for folks who want the bridge from Daughter of Fortune to The House of the Spirits, truly enjoy character-driven fiction and/or are interested in Chilean history. For those who like more plot-driven fiction, this will be a disappointment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8885127874754707145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570769781" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570769781" />
    <a title="Andy Wilkins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8879301-andy-wilkins"><img alt="Andy Wilkins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345520072p2/8879301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570769781?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy Wilkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy Wilkins" href="/user/show/8879301-andy-wilkins">Andy Wilkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570769781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14983962528412503762">This sequel to Daughters of Fortune pretty much carries on the same way as its predecessor. I had a feeling through the book of here's a bunch of characters previously created with a few new ones and then some of them do a bit of this, some of them do a bit of that. There wasn't such a strong central theme as there was in Daughters of Fortune. There also seemed too many serendipitous meetings which struck me as a too obvious method of reintroducing characters into the mix. One other thing that I</span>
  <span id="freeText14983962528412503762" style="display:none">This sequel to Daughters of Fortune pretty much carries on the same way as its predecessor. I had a feeling through the book of here's a bunch of characters previously created with a few new ones and then some of them do a bit of this, some of them do a bit of that. There wasn't such a strong central theme as there was in Daughters of Fortune. There also seemed too many serendipitous meetings which struck me as a too obvious method of reintroducing characters into the mix. One other thing that I didn't like too much which I think is common in historical novel is how the main characters seems to bring C20th thinking an philosophy to a different time period. Aurora does not seem to have any prejudice and seems unrealistically enlightened.<br /><br />Though the story is clearly inferior to the first book, the characters and intrigues more than held my attention. I liked reading about how the characters developed and how they interacted with events in history. Despite the great number of characters, Allende is masterful in her ability to bring them all to life. She also inspires me to read more of the historical events that touch her characters' lives and even visit Chile! As with the other books I've read by Allende, a real page turner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14983962528412503762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_969965710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/969965710" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406686-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292206847p2/406686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/969965710?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/406686-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer969965710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9236882401280299970">Vividly drawn characters, a hallmark of Allende’s that I’ve enjoyed before, take the forefront of this saga continuing the story of the extended Del Valle family, set largely in Chile during a time of tumult and in San Francisco of the post-Gold Rush times. It stands alone well, but if you’ve read House of the Spirits and Daughter of Fortune, both highly recommended, you’ll know some of the characters’ backgrounds better. Allende is particularly deft at bringing to life complex women who questio</span>
  <span id="freeText9236882401280299970" style="display:none">Vividly drawn characters, a hallmark of Allende’s that I’ve enjoyed before, take the forefront of this saga continuing the story of the extended Del Valle family, set largely in Chile during a time of tumult and in San Francisco of the post-Gold Rush times. It stands alone well, but if you’ve read House of the Spirits and Daughter of Fortune, both highly recommended, you’ll know some of the characters’ backgrounds better. Allende is particularly deft at bringing to life complex women who question or openly flaunt the prevailing mores of their society and times, and matriarchs stand out, but are secondary to the first person teller Aurora here. Several of the men take form well, too, for this reader, Severo and Williams and Chi’en. Various cultures in Chile and San Francisco are handled lovingly and even-handedly, in spite of the cultural strata of the late nineteenth century in both locales. Terrific characters in a most compelling story. You’ll be glad you read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9236882401280299970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1532249294249047829">I'm a fan of Allende's writing and though this book is different from any I've read so far, I loved it. One big constant with her, is her ability to create fascinating characters, and here the book doesn't disappoint. In contrast, the narrator seems somewhat wet behind the ears. But the whole canvas of the book makes for fascinating reading as it sweeps from California to Chile, on to Europe and back to Chile, and from one generation's story to the other. This book is not strong on plot. Indeed,</span>
  <span id="freeText1532249294249047829" style="display:none">I'm a fan of Allende's writing and though this book is different from any I've read so far, I loved it. One big constant with her, is her ability to create fascinating characters, and here the book doesn't disappoint. In contrast, the narrator seems somewhat wet behind the ears. But the whole canvas of the book makes for fascinating reading as it sweeps from California to Chile, on to Europe and back to Chile, and from one generation's story to the other. This book is not strong on plot. Indeed, by its very nature (a fictional memoire) it often gives glimpses in advance of what is going to happen later. But this does not spoil the pleasure of reading.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2285095340" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2285095340" />
    <a title="Carla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1705878-carla"><img alt="Carla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510758705p2/1705878.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2285095340?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carla" href="/user/show/1705878-carla">Carla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9722905325" href="/book/show/5983145-retrato-a-s-pia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=intense">intense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=angst">angst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=em-portugues">em-portugues</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=marriage">marriage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=historic">historic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=tears">tears</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1705878-carla?shelf=est%C3%A1gio">estágio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2285095340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6191011386938457026">When reading a book I'm usually all about the characters, a strong narrative and interesting world can usually be a bit more lacking if the characters can completely grab a hold of my attention. <br/><br/>“<b>Only passionate people make interesting characters.</b> Nice people with common sense only make good former spouses”. [Allende]<br/><br/>This book surprised me, in that aspect. This is not something I picked up out of my own volition, but it quickly had all of my attention. The premise is simple enough, we get to und</span>
  <span id="freeText6191011386938457026" style="display:none">When reading a book I'm usually all about the characters, a strong narrative and interesting world can usually be a bit more lacking if the characters can completely grab a hold of my attention. <br /><br />“<b>Only passionate people make interesting characters.</b> Nice people with common sense only make good former spouses”. [Allende]<br /><br />This book surprised me, in that aspect. This is not something I picked up out of my own volition, but it quickly had all of my attention. The premise is simple enough, we get to understand how Aurora came to be Aurora and all that she went through and who she went through it <i>with</i>. The book has an amazing fan of characters - the grandparents, uncles, parents, family friends, doctors, etc. You so easily get enraptured in each of their lives that it's easy to forget that this will all, ultimately, lead to Aurora. <br />Severo, Nívea, Paulina and Tao Chi'en specifically stood out for me - much like they did for Aurora, really. All, in very different ways, molded her and helped her grow and develop. Their presence in her childhood, her early teens and early years as an adult are a defining part of her life. It's impossible - well not impossible, but it would make this one a too long review - to properly explain how amazing it is the role each character played, and how in being a part of someone's life, they were someone themselves and that comes across perfectly clear. For a good chunk of the book I did feel like I was reading about Severo and that this was his journey; in a way, it was too. <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Tao Chi'en after suffering the loss of his first wife and after remarrying (Eliza Sommers) had two children: Ebanizer (Lucky) and Lynn. Lynn was named after Tao's first wife, Lin, but the writing was changed so she would have good luck. As it were, she was a very beautiful but incredibly naive woman, who really didn't understand much of how the world around her worked. Lynn would be the mother of Aurora. Matías, Paulina del Valle's eldest son was the biological father, but always refused even having ever been involved with Lynn and as such refused to acknowledge the child. It was Severo, who at the time - despite technically also being infatuated and in love with Nívea - was living with his aunt Paulina and fell in great love with Lynn, who offered to legally be the child's father and give her his family name which would guarantee her fortune. <br />With Lynn's untimely death, Tao and Eliza preferred to raise the child themselves.<br /><br />So Severo goes back to Nívea and the two of them have over a dozen children. I was happy for those two, lmao. He survived through war, amputation, dealt with loss. Nívea kept checking in on how things were, she kept herself informed about the world and kept fighting for women to have more rights - all of this she shared with Severo and they always traded books back and forth. They were lovely and went through a lot. Also her name fits her perfectly. But with this, and seeing as they build their own (extremely large) family, Severo and Nívea can't take Aurora in themselves because they wouldn't be able to give her all the attention she needs. So, since she's legally a del Valle, she goes to her paternal grandmother's house - Paulina del Valle. I love this woman. <i>So much</i>. Despite Aurora's love for her grandfather Tao, I can definitely say that Paulina shaped her and made her as strong of character as she is. Everyone saw her as an unshakeable force of nature, and that she was. A smart, business oriented woman who knew what she wanted and how she wanted. She was proper but extravagant. And for her, Aurora was her daughter.<br /><br />What put Aurora through a real struggle and shook her inside and out was her (pretty much forced) wedding to Diego - who she fell in puppy love with. Despite how she claims to have forgiven him, and how we're given light into what's happening with him I can't really come to feel sympathy for the man. He took a young girl and treated her as nobody should be. She was his beard and there wasn't really ever an effort to make her feel like anything else. Diego and Susana get 0 mercy. <br /><br />We could delve into the depth of how they acted as they did, and why Aurora understood them, and how it must've been hard for the two of them too - like I'm sure it was -, it's still super selfish to knowingly directly hurt two people (Eduardo - Susana's husband and Diego's brother -, and Aurora) and create the possibility of wrecking a whole family with three children involved. Like, nah. I don't respect that. <br /><br />Through the loss of family and love, and then regaining her foundations and finding her place is how Aurora finds out who she is as an individual. <br /><br />It's an incredible journey through someone's life, so beautifully explained and narrated that it's impossible not to feel how palpable and alive all of these characters are. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6191011386938457026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2195243186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2195243186" />
    <a title="Mrs. Elaine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16316375-mrs-elaine"><img alt="Mrs. Elaine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474639195p2/16316375.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195243186?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mrs. Elaine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mrs. Elaine" href="/user/show/16316375-mrs-elaine">Mrs. Elaine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16316375-mrs-elaine?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195243186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6386198147209855426">This is the second book I have completed by this author and I am currently reading a third. And what a wonderful writer she is. What struck me the most was her ability to make the protagonist the focal point of the story. I’m sure that sounds redundant and not in the least original but there is actually so much going on within the story that you can’t help but appreciate her ability not to become lost in the excess. Her novel(s) do contain romance but it never becomes the focal point, it remains</span>
  <span id="freeText6386198147209855426" style="display:none">This is the second book I have completed by this author and I am currently reading a third. And what a wonderful writer she is. What struck me the most was her ability to make the protagonist the focal point of the story. I’m sure that sounds redundant and not in the least original but there is actually so much going on within the story that you can’t help but appreciate her ability not to become lost in the excess. Her novel(s) do contain romance but it never becomes the focal point, it remains a part of the woman’s journey and I loved that. The other characters: lovers, family, friends, and business associates are but her accessories. Some break, some are lost, some remain with her for a lifetime but in the end they are all her accessories. <br /><br />It’s the woman, her thoughts, her passions, her explorations both into the world and of herself that makes the story. The readers are left with a living breathing work of art. Flawed, beautiful, and made more beautiful by those flaws. This is where Allende blew me away. Even the side characters become detailed works of art and we the readers are shown portraits of humanity in all its greatness and flaws. Some actions we recoil from, in others we see ourselves revealed on paper, and some we rejoice in. <br /><br />All of her books in my experience have a focal point of feminism. Not twentieth century feminism but the seeds that sparked it. You won’t find any bra burning or woman picketing for equal pay. The characters struggles remain limited to the times which I was grateful for. The author was exquisitely careful to remain true to the times. Nothing ruins an historical novel faster then characters behaving hundreds of years before their times in wholly unbelievable ways.<br /><br />There are multiple settings in her books. This one takes place in Chile and California. We jump from setting to setting as the characters move from place to place and the author gives you a good sense of the changes that are place from one location to another. <br /><br />In short, Allende tells a good solid story with good solid characters. I will be recommending her books frequently as I read more and more of her books. My only issue was finding myself wanting more. We aren’t left with cliffhangers but I could easily have read a hundred pages more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6386198147209855426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10023816695006570409">Celebrating Grandparents<br/><br/>If the power of the pen is to influence, and it is, this historical saga is a mighty sword! Beginning in 1860's revolutionary Chile, just before its separation from Spain in 1910, we travel to San Francisco, where Aurora, an orphan, is scooped up by the love of her grandparents. Her doting Chinese grandfather, Tao Chi'en, a physician, secretly rescues terrified children, transported in boat cages from China, to be sex-trafficked by exploiters in San Fran's China town. Aur</span>
  <span id="freeText10023816695006570409" style="display:none">Celebrating Grandparents<br /><br />If the power of the pen is to influence, and it is, this historical saga is a mighty sword! Beginning in 1860's revolutionary Chile, just before its separation from Spain in 1910, we travel to San Francisco, where Aurora, an orphan, is scooped up by the love of her grandparents. Her doting Chinese grandfather, Tao Chi'en, a physician, secretly rescues terrified children, transported in boat cages from China, to be sex-trafficked by exploiters in San Fran's China town. Aurora's two strong grandmothers, Eliza and Pauline, honor their grandchild's life by being their unswerving, self-directed selves. Their powerful example guides Aurora's to claim her own hard-earned autonomy. <br />Eleanor Cowan, author of : <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20735536.A_History_of_a_Pedophile_s_Wife_Memoir_of_a_Canadian_Teacher_and_Writer" title="A History of a Pedophile's Wife Memoir of a Canadian Teacher and Writer by Eleanor Cowan" rel="nofollow noopener">A History of a Pedophile's Wife: Memoir of a Canadian Teacher and Writer</a></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2217518357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9520256247789646392">1.5/5<br/><br/>Not sure what all the hoopla is about regarding Allende; if this novel is indicative of her work, I'm not at all interested. I had no investment in any of the characters (I should have known when I saw the book started with a family tree), the saga feeling was forced, the plot was lackluster, the pacing was strange, the structure was nonsensical, and the "drama" was tepid and dull. For a photographer's story, it was remarkably unphotogenic: nothing vivid, remarkable, or striking about the w</span>
  <span id="freeText9520256247789646392" style="display:none">1.5/5<br /><br />Not sure what all the hoopla is about regarding Allende; if this novel is indicative of her work, I'm not at all interested. I had no investment in any of the characters (I should have known when I saw the book started with a family tree), the saga feeling was forced, the plot was lackluster, the pacing was strange, the structure was nonsensical, and the "drama" was tepid and dull. For a photographer's story, it was remarkably unphotogenic: nothing vivid, remarkable, or striking about the whole novel. For an example of a superb dreamlike, episodic, "photo album" novel of a young woman's coming of age, readers should look to Duras' The Lover (L'Amant).<br /><br />The only nice thing I can say about it is that I learned something about Chilean history, I guess.</span>

  <span id="freeText190319754928105957" style="display:none">I didn't get really into this book until the final 100-ish pages, because that's how long the story takes to start focusing on the life of the narrator instead of her family's tangled history, so unfortunately I spent most of the book wondering why I was supposed to care about these characters. One of the main themes of the book is memory and keeping people's stories alive, but I felt that the narrator's search to unlock the memory of the early years of her life could have been written in a more captivating way.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kZMykM9fw0DzQNHWNA553O6z0zFsLMRluYg66RpVjuZb9GGgj0jsL9rOjwrGj/gMeOUW/q5JXZHvdWcOmc7aPw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5723571231970928276">Isabel Allende Llona is a Chilean-American novelist. Allende, who writes in the "magic realism" tradition, is considered one of the first successful women novelists in Latin America. She has written novels based in part on her own experiences, often focusing on the experiences of women, weaving myth and realism together. She has lectured and done extensive book tours and has taught literature at s</span>
  <span id="freeText5723571231970928276" style="display:none">Isabel Allende Llona is a Chilean-American novelist. Allende, who writes in the "magic realism" tradition, is considered one of the first successful women novelists in Latin America. She has written novels based in part on her own experiences, often focusing on the experiences of women, weaving myth and realism together. She has lectured and done extensive book tours and has taught literature at several US colleges. She currently resides in California with her husband. Allende adopted U.S. citizenship in 2003.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5723571231970928276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
